Sports Medicine (Auckland, N.Z.)2009;39(6):491-511."), [101](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0101 "KroghJ , NordentoftM , SterneJ , LawlorDA . The effect of exercise in clinical depressed adults: systematic review and meta-analysis of randomised controlled trials. Journal of Clinical Psychiatry2011;72(4):529-38."), [102](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0102 "LawlorDA , HopkerSW . The effectiveness of exercise as an intervention in the management of depression: systematic review and meta-regression analysis of randomised controlled trials. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2001;322(7289):763-7."), [103](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0103 "SjostenN , KivelaSL . The effects of physical exercise on depressive symptoms among the aged: a systematic review. International Journal of Geriatric Psychiatry2006;21(5):410-8.")\], whereas others included a range of different randomised and non‐randomised controlled studies and uncontrolled studies \[[49](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0049 "SilveiraH , MoraesH , OliveiraN , CoutinhoES , LaksJ , DeslandesA . Physical exercise and clinically depressed patients: a systematic review and meta-analysis. Neuropsychobiology2013;67(2):61-8."), [104](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0104 "NorthTC , McCullaghP , TranZV . Effect of exercise on depression. Exercise and Sport Sciences Reviews1990;18:379-415."), [105](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0105 "PinquartM , DubersteinPR , LynessJM . Effects of psychotherapy and other behavioral interventions on clinical depressed older adults: a meta-analysis. Aging & Mental Health2007;11(6):645-57."), [106](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0106 "BlakeH . How effective are physical activity interventions for alleviating depressive symptoms in older people? A systematic review. Clinical Rehabilitation2009;23:873-87."), [107](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0107 "CraftLL , LandersDM . The effect of exercise on clinical depression and depression resulting from mental illness: a meta-analysis. Journal of Sport & Exercise Psychology1998;20(4):339-57.")\]. Inadequacies in allocation concealment, blinding, sample size and intention‐to‐treat analysis have been identified within studies included in the systematic reviews \[[55](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0055 "JosefssonT , LindwallM , ArcherT . Physical exercise intervention in depressive disorders: meta-analysis and systematic review. Scandinavian Journal of Medicine & Science in Sports2014;24(2):259-72."), [99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]"), [100](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0100 "RethorstCD , WipfliBM , LandersDM . The antidepressive effects of exercise. Sports Medicine (Auckland, N.Z.)2009;39(6):491-511."), [101](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0101 "KroghJ , NordentoftM , SterneJ , LawlorDA . The effect of exercise in clinical depressed adults: systematic review and meta-analysis of randomised controlled trials. Journal of Clinical Psychiatry2011;72(4):529-38."), [102](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0102 "LawlorDA , HopkerSW . The effectiveness of exercise as an intervention in the management of depression: systematic review and meta-regression analysis of randomised controlled trials. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2001;322(7289):763-7.")\], raising the likelihood that results may have been affected \[[55](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0055 "JosefssonT , LindwallM , ArcherT . Physical exercise intervention in depressive disorders: meta-analysis and systematic review. Scandinavian Journal of Medicine & Science in Sports2014;24(2):259-72.")\]. Several meta‐analyses have shown the influence of methodological rigour on estimates of pooled effect size, with statistically significant benefits from treatment with exercise for depression disappearing when focusing on subgroups of more rigorous studies \[[91](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0091 "KroghJ , HjorthøjC , SpeyerH , GluudC , NordentoftM . Exercise for patients with major depression: a systematic review with meta-analysis and trial sequential analysis. BMJ Open2017;7(9):e014820. [DOI: 10.1136/bmjopen-2016-014820]"), [99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]"), [101](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0101 "KroghJ , NordentoftM , SterneJ , LawlorDA . The effect of exercise in clinical depressed adults: systematic review and meta-analysis of randomised controlled trials. Journal of Clinical Psychiatry2011;72(4):529-38.")\].

Given the heterogeneous nature of the evidence base assessing exercise as a treatment for depression, it is important to be transparent and consistent about the different decisions made regarding the methods that have been adopted. Also, it is important to recognise that differences will exist between different systematic reviews and meta‐analyses, interpreting the results within the context of the evidence presented. This review has continued to evolve, having originally been published in 2001 in the British Medical Journal \[[102](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0102 "LawlorDA , HopkerSW . The effectiveness of exercise as an intervention in the management of depression: systematic review and meta-regression analysis of randomised controlled trials. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2001;322(7289):763-7.")\], converted to a Cochrane review in 2008 \[[99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]")\] and updated in 2012 \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]")\] and 2013 \[[108](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0108 "RimerJ , DwanK , LawlorDA , GreigCA , McMurdoM , MorleyW , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2012, Issue 7. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub5]")\]. Although the current update broadly adheres to the protocol agreed with the Cochrane Depression, Anxiety and Neurosis Review Group (CCDAN) as outlined in previous versions, it has addressed issues raised since the previous version which necessitate consideration \[[43](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0043 "EkkekakisP . Honey, I shrunk the pooled SMD! Guide to critical appraisal of systematic reviews and meta-analyses using the Cochrane review on exercise for depression as example. Mental Health and Physical Activity2015;8:21-36."), [51](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0051 "SchuchFB , VancampfortD , RichardsJ , RosenbaumS , WardPB , StubbsB . Exercise as a treatment for depression: a meta-analysis adjusting for publication bias. Journal of Psychiatric Research2016;77:42-51."), [55](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0055 "JosefssonT , LindwallM , ArcherT . Physical exercise intervention in depressive disorders: meta-analysis and systematic review. Scandinavian Journal of Medicine & Science in Sports2014;24(2):259-72.")\]. Our intention in this review is to update the evidence base and to address any issues that have arisen in the assessment of the effectiveness of exercise for treating depression. Changes made to the scope of the review and the methods used are described below under ‘Differences between protocol and review’.

To determine the effectiveness of exercise in the treatment of depression in adults compared with no intervention, waiting list control or placebo, or where exercise is used as an adjunct to an established treatment that is received by both exercising and non‐exercising groups.

To determine the effectiveness of exercise compared with other active interventions for depression in adults (psychological therapies, pharmacological treatments or alternative interventions such as light therapy).

We followed the Methodological Expectations of Cochrane Intervention Reviews (MECIR) \[[109](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0109 "HigginsJ , LassersonT , ThomasJ , FlemyngE , ChurchillR . Standards for the conduct of new Cochrane Intervention Reviews, and the planning and conduct of updates. Methodological Expectations of Cochrane Intervention Reviews (MECIR). Available at https://www.cochrane.org/authors/handbooks-and-manuals/mecir-manual2023.")\] when conducting the review and reported the review using PRISMA 2020 \[[110](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0110 "PageMJ , McKenzieJE , BossuytPM , BoutronI , HoffmannTC , Mulrow CD et al. The PRISMA 2020 statement: an updated guideline for reporting systematic reviews. BMJ2021;372:n71. [DOI: 10.1136/bmj.n71]")\].

### Differences between protocol and review

#### Changes for this update (2025 version)

- We performed an additional sensitivity analysis to explore the effect of excluding those trials for which we used the arm with the largest clinical effect rather than the largest 'dose' of exercise.
- We specified a new analysis that assessed 'other' interventions, which included bright light therapy, reflexology, occupational or art therapy, supplements and health education.
- Some studies with a control arm that had been specified as placebo in previous versions of the review have been reclassified as specific active therapies in this 2025 update.
- We modified our categorisation of the intensity of exercise in the studies to align with ACSM criteria.
- We undertook new subgroup analyses to look at the effects of exercise when given as an adjunct.

### Criteria for considering studies for this review

#### Types of studies

We included randomised controlled trials (RCTs), including parallel, cluster, or individual, or the first phase of cross‐over trials. We defined a trial as an RCT if the allocation of participants to intervention and comparison groups was described as randomised (including terms such as 'randomly', 'random' or 'randomisation').

#### Types of participants

##### Participant characteristics

Adult men and women aged 18 and over (with no upper age limit) in any setting, including inpatients. We included studies if the participants were defined by the author of the trial as having depression (by any method of diagnosis and of any severity). We excluded trials that randomised people both with and without depression, even if results from the subgroups of participants with depression were reported separately, in keeping with what was done in previous versions of this review \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]"), [99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]"), [108](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0108 "RimerJ , DwanK , LawlorDA , GreigCA , McMurdoM , MorleyW , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2012, Issue 7. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub5]")\].

##### Diagnosis

The effects of exercise on depressive symptoms in participants with emotional distress (but not fulfilling a diagnosis of depression) or those who are healthy were not included in this review. We acknowledge that it can sometimes be difficult to distinguish between depression and dysthymia in the 'real world', as there need be only two weeks of decreased interest and enjoyment to diagnose depression. However, we were primarily interested in the role of exercise in people with depression for whom there is substantial morbidity rather than in people with mild, transient episodes of low mood.

We did not include studies that investigated the effect of exercise on anxiety and neurotic disorders, dysthymia (i.e. low mood not fulfilling diagnostic criteria for depression) or postnatal depression.

##### Comorbidities

We did not exclude studies or participants based on comorbidities.

##### Setting

Any setting was eligible for inclusion in the review.

#### Types of interventions

We defined exercise as “planned, structured and repetitive bodily movement done to improve or maintain one or more components of physical fitness” \[[46](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0046 "American College of Sports Medicine. ACSM's Resource Manual for Guidelines for Exercise Testing and Prescription. 4th edition. Lippincott, Williams and Wilkins, 2001.")\]. This definition has been applied since the previous update \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]")\] and it was agreed to continue its use for future updates by the CCDAN Review Group editorial team. We also excluded studies that assessed the provision of advice on how to increase physical activity that does not fulfil the ACSM definition of exercise.

##### Experimental intervention

Any type of exercise (as defined above). We excluded studies that measured outcomes immediately before and after a single exercise session, and trials that provided less than a week of exercise.

##### Comparator intervention

We included studies in which exercise was compared to no intervention, a 'waiting list control' or placebo (defined by study authors); and those in which exercise was used as an adjunct to an established treatment (which was received (in an identical way) by participants in both the exercising and non‐exercising group, e.g. exercise plus cognitive behavioural therapy (CBT) versus CBT alone).

We also included other types of active treatments, where the aim of the treatment was to improve mood. This included pharmacological treatments, psychological therapies or other alternative treatments. Where a study identified a comparator as placebo and stated it was an active treatment, whether pharmacological, psychological or other therapies, we classified it as the specified active therapy.

Although this strategy was the same as that included in the original review \[[102](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0102 "LawlorDA , HopkerSW . The effectiveness of exercise as an intervention in the management of depression: systematic review and meta-regression analysis of randomised controlled trials. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2001;322(7289):763-7.")\], some studies that were specified as placebo in previous versions have been reclassified as specific active therapies in this 2025 update. We made this change only when the interventions were clearly stated and the comparator was included in an analysis.

We excluded studies that compared two different types of exercise but had no non‐exercising comparison group.

We excluded studies described by the authors as 'combination treatments', where exercise was one component of the combination, because we could not disentangle the effect of exercise from the effect of the other components of the intervention.

### Outcome measures

#### Critical outcomes

- Our primary outcome was a measure of depression or mood, either continuous or dichotomous. Continuous measures of depression were reported using a variety of depression scales, the most common of which were the Beck Depression Inventory \[[111](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0111 "BeckAT , WardCH , MendelsonM , MockJ , ErbaughJ . An inventory for measuring depression. Archives of General Psychiatry1961;4(6):561-71.")\] and the Hamilton Rating Scale for Depression \[[112](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0112 "HamiltonM . A rating scale for depression. Journal of Neurology, Neurosurgery and Psychiatry1960;23:56-62.")\].

In previous versions of the review, where trials used a number of different tools to assess depression, we included only the main outcome measure in the meta‐analysis. We defined the main outcome measure using a hierarchy of criteria: i) identified by the trial authors as the main outcome measure, ii) outcome reported in the abstract, iii) first outcome reported in the Results section.

Where trials used dichotomous data for primary outcomes, and also provided data on continuous outcome measures, we used the data provided in the trial reports for the continuous outcome measure in our meta‐analysis. This was because we knew from previous updates that most trials reported only continuous outcomes.

##### Timing of outcome assessment

We extracted data at the end of treatment and also at the end of any longer‐term follow‐up after the intervention had stopped.

#### Important outcomes

- Acceptability of treatment, assessed by a) attendance at exercise interventions, and b) the number of participants completing the interventions
- Quality of life (e.g. SF‐36 Health Survey \[[113](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0113 "Ware JE Jr,  SherbourneCD . The MOS 36-item short-form health survey (SF-36). I. Conceptual framework and item selection. Medical Care1992;30(6):473-83.")\], health‐related quality of life)
- Cost (e.g. average treatment cost, health service costs)
- Adverse events (e.g. musculoskeletal pain, fatigue)
- In order to better understand the generalisability of exercise for depression, we also extracted data on the number of people screened for inclusion and the number recruited ([Table 1](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-tbl-0005)).

[Open in table viewer](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#0)

BDI: Beck Depression Inventory

### Search methods for identification of studies

#### Electronic searches

We carried out the following electronic searches ([Supplementary material 1](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0001)).

- The Cochrane Depression, Anxiety and Neurosis Review Group's Specialised Register (CCDANCTR) (February 2010 to February 2016)
- The Cochrane Central Register of Controlled Trials (CENTRAL), in the Cochrane Library (February 2010 to November 2023)
- MEDLINE Ovid (February 2010 to November 2023)
- EMBASE Ovid (February 2010 to November 2023)
- PsycINFO (February 2010 to November 2023)

The searches of MEDLINE, Embase, PsycINFO and CENTRAL between 2010 and 2016 were encompassed within our searches of CCDANCTR, which included these databases. Also, we searched the ISRCTN Registry (in March 2013, January 2017, February 2019, November 2020, July 2021 and September 2025) to identify any ongoing trials. We performed an electronic search of ClinicalTrials.gov and WHO International Clinical Trials Registry Platform (ICTRP) in March 2013, January 2017, February 2019, November 2020, July 2021 and September 2025. An RCT filter developed by CCDAN was used.

Because the searches for the CDCANCTR register were kept up‐to‐date and comprehensive, we were advised by the CCDAN editorial team that it was not necessary to search the other databases until the November 2019 update search.

We searched for errata and retractions for the included studies by searching for the papers on MEDLINE and Embase, and checking those records, updates on the publisher’s website and the Retraction Watch database.

Previous updates of the review provide details of the search strategy prior to the searches conducted for the current update \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]"), [99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]"), [108](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0108 "RimerJ , DwanK , LawlorDA , GreigCA , McMurdoM , MorleyW , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2012, Issue 7. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub5]"), [114](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0114 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2008, Issue 4. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub3]")\].

#### Searching other resources

Due to limitations in available resources for this current update, we did not repeat handsearching conducted in previous updates. We contacted authors only to obtain any missing information about trial details (e.g. standard deviations).

We screened the bibliographies of all included articles for additional references.

### Data collection and analysis

#### Selection of studies

We selected studies through a two‐stage process. The full literature search results were independently screened by pairs of review authors (JH, DM, CL, AC, CH, CS, KD), identifying all those citations that may meet the selection criteria. Full manuscripts of all selected citations were then retrieved and assessed independently by the pairs of review authors against the study selection criteria. Any differences in opinion were resolved through consensus or, if necessary, by arbitration by a third review author who was independent of the original decision. For the searches of the CCDANCTR up to February 2020, their Trials Search Co‐ordinator/Information Specialist checked abstracts, excluded obviously irrelevant ones, and then sent a list of the remaining citations to the review authors for scrutiny. We created a PRISMA flow diagram to outline the study selection process.

#### Data extraction and management

We extracted data, when available, at the end of treatment and at the end of follow‐up. For this update, seven review authors (JH, DM, AC, CH, CS, CL, KD) independently extracted data for our primary and secondary outcomes for the new trials identified. All authors, working in pairs, verified extracted data from a subset of included studies. If differences in opinion occurred, these were resolved by discussion, or, if necessary, through recourse to a third review author. The data we extracted were participants, interventions, outcome measures, results, number of people screened, number randomised, number allocated to exercise, number who dropped out of the exercise arm ([Table 1](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-tbl-0005)), secondary clinical outcomes, cost, adverse events and main conclusions. All the review authors used the same structured paper extraction form, which had been piloted.

In addition, we extracted data on the type of exercise from all the included trials, to enable a categorisation of the intensity of exercise according to ACSM criteria. This was undertaken by one author (JH) and checked by a second author (DM or AC). In categorising the intensity levels in this updated version of the review, we made a readjustment to maintain the five‐intensity‐level categorisation stated in the ACSM criteria \[[115](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0115 "GarberCE , BlissmerB , DeschenesMR , FranklinBA , LamonteMJ , LeeIM , et al. American College of Sports Medicine position stand. Quantity and quality of exercise for developing and maintaining cardiorespiratory, musculoskeletal, and neuromotor fitness in apparently healthy adults: guidance for prescribing exercise. Medicine and Science in Sports and Exercise2011;43(7):1334-59.")\], which are very light, light, moderate, vigorous and near maximum to maximum. Wherever possible, we used direct measures to estimate the intensity levels (percentage of heart rate reserve, percentage of oxygen uptake reserve, percentage of maximum heart rate and one‐maximum‐repetition percentage) \[[116](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0116 "HowleyET . Type of activity: resistance, aerobic and leisure versus occupational physical activity. Medicine & Science in Sports & Exercise2001;33(6):s419-20. [DOI: 10.1097/00005768-200106001-00005]")\]. Where this was not available, an indirect measure was utilised. If available, the classification from the author was used; if not, a classification of the type of exercise metabolic equivalent (METs) was derived from the Compendium of Physical Activities ([https://sites.google.com/site/compendiumofphysicalactivities/Activity](https://sites.google.com/site/compendiumofphysicalactivities/Activity)). All measurements of intensity were then cross‐referenced to approximate the classification of exercise intensity ACSM criteria \[[115](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0115 "GarberCE , BlissmerB , DeschenesMR , FranklinBA , LamonteMJ , LeeIM , et al. American College of Sports Medicine position stand. Quantity and quality of exercise for developing and maintaining cardiorespiratory, musculoskeletal, and neuromotor fitness in apparently healthy adults: guidance for prescribing exercise. Medicine and Science in Sports and Exercise2011;43(7):1334-59.")\]. For the MET values, when given, age was taken into consideration using the mean age (if reported). Wherever possible, we calculated 'triangulation of intensity levels'. If multiple intensity levels were used throughout the session/intervention, the programme intensity that represented most of the session/intervention programme was classified as the intervention intensity level. Where multiple direct and indirect conflicting measurements were given for relative intensity levels, we used the direct measure to classify the exercise intensity compared to indirect measures. Where a range of direct or indirect measures were given that crossed two levels of intensity, the intensity level was set where most of the overlap occurred. Where two intensity levels were described within the intervention, we selected the intensity level that represented most of the session or programme as the overall intensity level.

##### Main comparisons

We undertook the following analyses.

- Exercise versus 'control' (as defined above)
- Exercise versus psychological therapies
- Exercise versus pharmacological treatments
- Exercise versus 'other' interventions (including bright light therapy, reflexology, occupational or art therapy, supplements, health education)

#### Risk of bias assessment in included studies

We used the Cochrane Collaboration risk of bias tool to assess risks of bias, according to Chapter 8 of the *Cochrane Handbook for* *Systematic Reviews of Interventions* \[[117](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0117 "HigginsJP , ThomasJ , ChandlerJ , CumpstonM , LiT , PageMJ , et al (editors). Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions version 6.0 (updated July 2019). Available from https://www.cochrane.org/handbook.")\]. Two review authors (from JH, AC, DM, CH, CS, CL, KD) independently extracted data from each study on random sequence generation, allocation concealment, blinding of participants, blinding of those delivering the intervention, blinding of outcome assessors, incomplete outcome data, selective reporting and other potential biases. We categorised each of these domains as being at high risk of bias, unclear risk of bias or low risk of bias. All authors met in pairs to check decisions. We resolved any differences through discussion—or recourse to a third review author when necessary.

For concealment of allocation, we distinguished between trials that were adequately concealed (central randomisation at a site remote from the study; computerised allocation in which records are in a locked, unreadable file that can be assessed only after entering participant details; the drawing of opaque envelopes), inadequately concealed (open lists or tables of random numbers; open computer systems; drawing of non‐opaque envelopes) and unclear (no information in report, and the authors either did not respond to requests for information or were unable to provide information).

Trials could only be defined as 'intention‐to‐treat' if participants were analysed according to the allocated treatment and all participants either completed allocated treatments or any missing outcome data were replaced using a recognised statistical method (e.g. last observation carried forward (LOCF)).

For blinding, we distinguished between trials in which the main outcome was measured by an assessor who was blind to treatment allocation (blind) and those in which the main outcome was measured either by the participants themselves (i.e. self report) or by a non‐blinded assessor (not blind).

#### Measures of treatment effect

We undertook a narrative review of all studies and a meta‐analysis of those studies with appropriate data. Where trials used a number of different tools to assess depression, we included the main outcome measure only in the meta‐analysis. The main outcome measure was defined using a hierarchy of criteria as follows: identified by the authors as the main outcome measure; outcome reported in the abstract; first outcome reported in the Results section. For continuous data where different scales were used, the standardised mean difference (SMD) was calculated and reported with a 95% confidence interval (CI). For dichotomous data, we calculated the risk ratio and reported this with a 95% CI.

We interpreted the SMDs using the following rule of thumb: 0.2 represents a small effect, 0.5 a moderate effect and 0.8 a large effect \[[118](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0118 "SchünemannHJ , OxmanAD , VistGE , HigginsJP , DeeksJJ , GlasziouP , et al. Chapter 12: Interpreting results and drawing conclusions. In: Higgins JP, Green S. Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions Version 5.1.0 (updated March 2011). The Cochrane Collaboration, 2011. Available from https://www.cochrane.handbook.org.")\]. We planned to pool follow‐up data from those trials that reassessed participants at the end of the intervention and at subsequent time points (long‐term follow‐up). Long‐term follow‐up could mean an assessment at any point of time (after the initial assessment) after the intervention had been completed.

#### Unit of analysis issues

##### Studies with multiple treatment groups

Where studies with multiple treatment groups included a control arm, an exercise arm and an 'established treatment' arm (e.g. CBT, antidepressants), we extracted data on exercise versus control, and exercise versus established treatment. This meant that data from the exercise arm were included in two separate comparisons. If trials compared an established treatment (e.g. CBT, antidepressants) versus exercise versus both the established treatment and exercise, we made two comparisons: (i) established treatment plus exercise versus established treatment alone, and included this in the meta‐analysis of treatment versus control; (ii) exercise versus established treatment (e.g. CBT, antidepressants). This means that data from the 'established treatment alone' arm was used in two separate comparisons. In the review versions in 2001 \[[102](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0102 "LawlorDA , HopkerSW . The effectiveness of exercise as an intervention in the management of depression: systematic review and meta-regression analysis of randomised controlled trials. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2001;322(7289):763-7.")\], 2009 \[[99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]")\] and 2012 \[[108](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0108 "RimerJ , DwanK , LawlorDA , GreigCA , McMurdoM , MorleyW , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2012, Issue 7. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub5]")\], for trials that included more than one intensity of exercise, we used the exercise arm with the greatest clinical effect. Similarly, when trials provided more than one type of exercise, we used the type of exercise with the greatest clinical effect. However, because this may overestimate the effect of exercise, in this and the previous update of the review \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]")\], the exercise arm that provides the biggest 'dose' of exercise was used, and we performed a sensitivity analysis to explore the effect of using the smallest dose.

##### Cross‐over trials

For cross‐over trials, we intended to use the first phase of the trial only due to the potential 'carry‐over' effect of exercise. To date, we have not included any cross‐over trials.

##### Cluster‐randomised trials

If we identified cluster‐randomised trials that were incorrectly analysed using individuals as the unit of analysis, we intended to make corrections using the intracluster correlation coefficient (ICC). If this had not been available, we would have imputed the ICC from similar studies. In fact, we did not find any cluster‐RCTs to include.

#### Dealing with missing data

In two previous versions of this review \[[99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]"), [102](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0102 "LawlorDA , HopkerSW . The effectiveness of exercise as an intervention in the management of depression: systematic review and meta-regression analysis of randomised controlled trials. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2001;322(7289):763-7.")\], we found current contact details of all authors through correspondence addresses in study reports and by searching websites. We contacted all authors by email or post (sending reminders to non‐responders) to establish missing details from the methods and results sections of the written reports and to determine authors’ knowledge of, or involvement in, any current work in the area. For the last two updates \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]"), [108](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0108 "RimerJ , DwanK , LawlorDA , GreigCA , McMurdoM , MorleyW , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2012, Issue 7. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub5]")\] and this 2025 update, we contacted authors only if there were missing data items, or if we needed more detail to determine whether to include the study.

Some trials, in which participants dropped out, reported data from only the remaining participants, so we used these data in our meta‐analyses. For trials that attempted to impute data from missing participants (e.g. LOCF for continuous data), we used the imputed values and categorised the trial as 'intention‐to‐treat'. When we could not obtain information either from the publication or from the authors, we classified the trial as 'not intention‐to‐treat', and used the data from the available cases in the meta‐analysis.

#### Reporting bias assessment

We used a funnel plot to explore reporting biases when 10 or more studies were included in the meta‐analysis. However, other reasons such as heterogeneity and small‐study effects also cause asymmetry in funnel plots \[[117](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0117 "HigginsJP , ThomasJ , ChandlerJ , CumpstonM , LiT , PageMJ , et al (editors). Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions version 6.0 (updated July 2019). Available from https://www.cochrane.org/handbook.")\].

#### Synthesis methods

We used a random‐effects model based on DerSimonian and Laird’s method to calculate the pooled effect size \[[119](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0119 "DerSimonianR , LairdN . Meta-analysis in clinical trials. Controlled Clinical Trials1986;7(3):177-88.")\], using a standardised mean difference where studies measured the same outcome but used different scales. Where studies used the same scales for the outcome or there was only one included study, we presented a mean difference. We synthesised data from trials where outcome data were collected as soon as the intervention ended, and performed a separate synthesis of data collected weeks or months after the intervention ended, to explore whether any benefits were retained after the intervention had been completed. With complex interventions, we had to decide whether the interventions were sufficiently similar to be combined into a meta‐analysis. We included trials that fulfilled the ACSM definition of exercise \[[46](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0046 "American College of Sports Medicine. ACSM's Resource Manual for Guidelines for Exercise Testing and Prescription. 4th edition. Lippincott, Williams and Wilkins, 2001.")\], and we combined these data in a meta‐analysis.

We used the Chi² test, together with the I² statistic, to assess heterogeneity.

A P value of 0.1 or less indicates significant heterogeneity when considering Chi². The ranges for I² are:

- 0% to 40%: might not be important;
- 30% to 60%: may represent moderate heterogeneity;
- 50% to 90%: may represent substantial heterogeneity;
- 75% to 100%: considerable heterogeneity.

Note that the importance of the observed value of I² depends on (i) the magnitude and direction of effects and (ii) the strength of evidence for heterogeneity (e.g. P value from the Chi² test, or a confidence interval for I²) \[[117](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0117 "HigginsJP , ThomasJ , ChandlerJ , CumpstonM , LiT , PageMJ , et al (editors). Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions version 6.0 (updated July 2019). Available from https://www.cochrane.org/handbook.")\].

#### Investigation of heterogeneity and subgroup analysis

We conducted subgroup analyses on studies comparing exercise versus control, with all subgroup analyses prespecified in the protocol used for the current and previous versions of the review.

- Type of exercise
	- In this update, like the previous update, we explored the effect of different types of exercise (aerobic, resistance exercise or mixed aerobic and resistance) for those trials comparing exercise versus control on the primary outcome of reduction in depression symptoms.
- Intensity of exercise
	- In this update, like the previous update, we explored the effects of the intensity of exercise on the primary outcome of reduction in depression symptoms through a subgroup analysis that subdivided intensity of exercise into very light, light, moderate, vigorous, near maximum to maximum.
- Number of exercise sessions
	- In this update, like the previous update, we explored the effect of the number of exercise sessions on reduction in depression symptoms, by extracting data on the length of the exercise programme and the frequency of exercise sessions. We categorised studies by the total number of sessions and then grouped the total number as 0 to 12, 13 to 24, 25 to 36, at least 37.
- Diagnosis of depression
	- In this update, like the previous update, we explored how the diagnosis of depression at baseline (using a cut‐point on a scale, or by psychiatric interview) influenced the effect of exercise on reduction in depressive symptoms at the end of treatment.
- Type of control
	- Given concerns raised about the potential additive effects of the use of exercise as an adjunct to an established treatment received in an identical way by participants in both exercising and non‐exercising groups (e.g. exercise plus CBT versus CBT alone), we undertook new subgroup analyses to look at these effects separately on the reduction in depressive symptoms. This was part of a subgroup analysis that encompassed the different types of control.

#### Sensitivity analysis

We undertook sensitivity analyses to explore how much of the variation between studies comparing exercise to no exercise is explained by between‐study differences of the following types.

- Publication type (peer‐reviewed journal, conference abstract/proceedings, doctoral dissertation)
- Allocation concealment
- Intention‐to‐treat analysis (as defined above)
- Blinding
- Treatment dose

We then performed a sensitivity analysis, as we had done previously, including trials that were at low risk of bias for all three key criteria: allocation concealment, intention‐to‐treat, blinding. We also performed a sensitivity analysis using those trials that had several arms, for which we had included the arm with the biggest 'dose' of exercise in the initial analysis. In this sensitivity analysis, we included the arm with the smallest dose.

#### Certainty of the evidence assessment

##### Summary of findings tables

We created summary of findings tables for the comparisons of exercise versus (1) all non‐active control types; (2) psychological therapy; (3) pharmacological treatment and (4) specific non‐active control types (subgroup analyses). We generated the tables in GRADEpro GDT software \[[120](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0120 "SchünemannH , BrożekJ , GuyattG , OxmanA , editors. GRADE handbook for grading quality of evidence and strength of recommendations. Updated October 2013. The GRADE Working Group. Available from https://guidelinedevelopment.org/handbook2013.")\]. The outcomes presented were depressive symptoms (post‐treatment and follow‐up), treatment completion, quality of life and adverse events. We evaluated the certainty of the estimates in the summary of findings tables using the GRADE approach \[[121](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0121 "GRADE Working Group. GRADE Handbook for Grading Quality of Evidence and Strength of Recommendations (updated 2013). GRADE Working Group, 2013. Available from https://book.gradepro.org/."), [122](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0122 "GuyattGH , OxmanAD , VistGE , KunzR , Falck-YtterY , Alonso-CoelloP , et al; GRADE Working Group. GRADE: an emerging consensus on rating quality of evidence and strength of recommendations. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2008;336(7650):924-6.")\]. We categorised the certainty of the evidence as high, moderate, low or very low, based on overall risk of bias, imprecision, inconsistency, indirectness and potential publication bias. Two review authors (JH, CH) independently assessed the certainty of the outcomes presented. In cases of disagreement, consensus was sought through discussion; if the disagreement was not resolved, a third review author (AC) provided arbitration.

### Consumer involvement

No patients or members of the public were involved in the development and conduct of the original review or its updates.

### Description of studies

#### Results of the search

The results of searches for the previous updates have been described in detail elsewhere \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]"), [99](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0099 "MeadGE , MorleyW , CampbellP , GreigCA , McMurdoM , LawlorDA . Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2009, Issue 3. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub4]"), [102](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0102 "LawlorDA , HopkerSW . The effectiveness of exercise as an intervention in the management of depression: systematic review and meta-regression analysis of randomised controlled trials. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)2001;322(7289):763-7."), [108](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0108 "RimerJ , DwanK , LawlorDA , GreigCA , McMurdoM , MorleyW , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2012, Issue 7. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub5]")\].

In the previous update \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]")\], the review authors undertook the full literature search in March 2013. They repeated the searches again on 2 May 2013, listing any potentially eligible studies as ‘studies awaiting classification’, which were to be considered in a subsequent update. Of the seven studies identified by Cooney and colleagues \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]")\] as ‘studies awaiting classification’ (Aghakhani 2011 \[[123](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0123 "AghakhaniN , SharifF , KhademvatanK , RahbarN , EghtedarS , Shojaei MotlaghV . The reduction in anxiety and depression by education of patients with myocardial infarction. International Cardiovascular Research Journal2011;5(2):66-8. "), [124](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0124 "Aghakhani N KhademvatanK , & DehghaniMR . Hiking in suicidal patients: Neutral effects on markers of suicidality. American Journal of Medicine2013;126(10):927-30. ")\]), none met the inclusion criteria for this update. We excluded five of these studies because participants did not have to have depression to enter the trial (Aghakhani 2011; Gotta 2012; Murphy 2012; Pinniger 2012; Sturm 2012); we excluded one study because participants had multiple pathologies (Martiny 2013); and we excluded the final study because the control group received flexibility exercises (DEMO II 2012).

Cooney and colleagues also identified 23 trials that were ongoing \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]")\]. Since then, 14 of these trials have been registered as completed. We excluded four of them from this update due to the participants not having depression on entering the trial (Kerse 2010 \[[145](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0145 "KerseN , FalloonK , MoyesSA , HaymanKJ , DowellT , KoltGS , et al. DeLLITE Depression in late life: An intervention trial of exercise. Design and recruitment of a randomised controlled trial [ACTRN12605000475640]. BMC Geriatrics2008;8:ArtID 12. "), [146](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0146 "KerseN , HaymanKJ , MoyesSA , PeriK , RobinsonE , DowellA , et al. Home-based activity program for older people with depressive symptoms: DeLLITE - a randomized controlled trial. Annals of Family Medicine2010;8:214-23. ")\] (ACTRN12605000475640); Rezaei 2015 \[[147](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0147 "RezaeiJ , AbdiA , RezaeiM , HeydarnezhadianJ , JalaliR . Effect of regular exercise program on depression in hemodialysis patients. International Scholarly Research Notices2015;2015:182030. [PMID: 27347502]")\] (IRCT201205159763); Pibernik‐Okanovic 2015 \[[148](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0148 "Pibernik-OkanovicM , HermannsN , AjdukovicD , KosJ , PrasekM , SekerijaM , et al. Does treatment of subsyndromal depression improve depression-related and diabetes-related outcomes? A randomised controlled comparison of psychoeducation, physical exercise and enhanced treatment as usual. Trials2015;16:305. [PMID: 26174334]")\] (ISRCTN05673017); Taylor‐Young 2011 \[[149](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0149 "Taylor-YoungP , HauserP , SchuldheisS , Winters-StoneK . Efficacy of an exercise intervention to decrease depressive symptoms in depressed veterans with hepatitis c. In: https://clinicaltrials.gov/study/NCT00643695. Kissimmee, Florida: University of South Florida College of Nursing, 2011. ")\] (NCT00643695)). Another seven trials, although showing as having been completed on trial registers, do not appear to have published their findings (NCT00931814 \[[150](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0150 "NCT00931814. ClinicalTrials.gov/show/NCT01464463 (accessed 1 March 2013). ")\]; UMIN000001488 \[[153](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0153 "UMIN000001488. ")\]; NCT01763983 \[[154](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0154 "NCT01763983. ")\]; ACTRN12612000094875 \[[155](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0155 "MackintoshS . Improving mood through physical activity for carers and care recipients (IMPACCT): protocol for a randomised trial. Journal of Physiotherapy2013;59(2):125. "), [156](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0156 "ACTRN12612000094875. ")\]). Although we contacted all primary trial investigators on these trials to request additional information (where contact details were available), we received no responses. Due to this, we classified them as ongoing, and they will be checked at the next update for linked publications. We judged the remaining three completed trials to have met the selection criteria and included them in this update (Pfaff 2014 \[[158](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0158 "PfaffJJ , AlfonsoH , NewtonRU , SimM , FlickerL , AlmeidaOP . [PMID: 23833045]")\] (ACTRN12609000150246); Barclay 2014 \[[159](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0159 "BarclayT , RichardsS , SchoffstallJ , MagnusonC , McPheeC , PriceJ , et al. A pilot study on the effects of exercise on depression symptoms using levels of neurotransmitters and EEG as markers. European Journal of Psychology and Educational Studies2014;1(1):30-5. ")\] (NCT01787201); Kruisdijk 2018 \[[160](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0160 "KruisdijkF , HendriksenI , TakE , Beekman A-J, Hopman-RockM . EFFORT-D study process evaluation: challenges in conducting a trial into the effects of running therapy in patients with major depressive disorder. Annals of General Psychiatry2018;17(10):1-10. "), [161](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0161 "KruisdijkF , Hopman-RockM , BeekmanAT , HendriksenIJ . International Journal of Psychiatry in Clinical Practice2020;24(4):380-6. EFFORT-D: results of a randomised controlled trial testing the EFFect of running therapy on depression. BMC Psychiatry2019;19(170):1-14. ")\] (NTR1894)). Of the remaining nine trials that were identified as ongoing by Cooney and colleagues \[[32](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0032 "CooneyGM , DwanK , GreigCA , LawlorDA , RimerJ , WaughFR , et al. Exercise for depression. Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews2013, Issue 9. Art. No: CD004366. [DOI: 10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6]")\], we excluded four trials as they did not meet the selection criteria: intervention groups in three trials used either yoga (Schneider 2016 \[[163](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0163 "SchneiderKL , PanzaE , HandschinB , MaY , BuschAM , WaringME , et al. Feasibility of pairing behavioral activation with exercise for women with type 2 diabetes and depression: the get it study pilot randomized controlled trial. Behavior Therapy2016;47(2):198-212. ")\] (NCT01024790)), self‐help (Ström 2013 \[[164](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0164 "StrömM , Uckelstam C-J, AnderssonG , HassménP , UmefjordG , CarlbringP . Internet-delivered therapist-guided physical activity for mild to moderate depression: a randomized controlled trial. Black K, Ed. PeerJ2013;1(178):.. ")\] (NCT01573130)) or a multimodal intervention with meditation as a warm‐up routine (Sadeghi 2016 \[[165](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0165 "SadeghiK , AhmadiSM , AhmadiSM , RezaeiM , MiriJ , AbdiA , et al. A comparative study of the efficacy of cognitive group therapy and aerobic exercise in the treatment of depression among the students. Global Journal of Health Science2016;8(10):54171. [PMID: 27302433]")\] (IRCT2012061910003N1)) and the control group in a fourth trial used exercise (Clark 2014 \[[166](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0166 "ClarkD . The role of exercise in depression among middle and older aged adults. Indiana University2014. ")\] (NCT01573728)). A further two trials have been terminated (NCT01401569 \[[167](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0167 "NCT01401569. Efficacy of exercise and counseling intervention on relapse in smoker with depressive disorders (STOB-ACTIV). https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT01401569. ")\]; NCT01805479 \[[168](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0168 "NCT01805479. Exercise training in depressed traumatic brain injury survivors. https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT01805479. ")\]), and we classified two trials as still ongoing (NCT01619930 \[[169](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0169 "CarlbringP , LindnerP , MartellC , HassménP , ForsbergL , StrömL , et al. The effects on depression of Internet-administered behavioural activation and physical exercise with treatment rationale and relapse prevention: study protocol for a randomised controlled trial. Trials2013;14:35. "), [170](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0170 "SvartvattenN , SegerlundM , DennhagI , AnderssonG , CarlbringP . A content analysis of client e-mails in guided internet-based cognitive behavior therapy for depression. Internet Interventions2015;2(2):121-7. "), [171](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0171 "NCT01619930. The effects of behavioural activation and physical exercise on depression. ClinicalTrials.gov/show/NCT01619930 (accessed 1 March 2013). ")\]; NCT01696201 \[[172](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0172 "NCT01696201. Effect of a supervised exercise program during whole pregnancy on outcomes and level of depression. ClinicalTrials.gov/show/NCT01696201 (accessed 1 March 2013). ")\]). We identified that another trial had been included in a previous update of the review (Krogh 2009 \[[173](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0173 "KroghJ , SaltinB , GluudC , NordentoftM . The DEMO Trial: a randomized, parallel-group, observer-blinded clinical trial of strength versus aerobic versus relaxation training for patients with mild to moderate depression. Journal of Clinical Psychiatry2009;70(6):790-800. "), [174](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0174 "KroghJ , NordentoftM , Mohammad-NezhadM , WestrinA . Growth hormone, prolactin and cortisol response to exercise in patients with depression. Journal of Affective Disorders2010;125:189-97. "), [175](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0175 "KroghJ , PetersenL , TimmermannM , SaltinB , NordentoftM . Design paper: the DEMO trial: a randomized, parallel-group, observer-blinded clinical trial of aerobic versus non-aerobic versus relaxation training for patients with light to moderate depression [NCT00103415]. Contemporary Clinical Trials2007;28(1):79-89. ")\] (NCT00103415)).

In February 2016, February 2019, November 2020 and November 2023, we replicated the original search of the Cochrane Depression, Anxiety and Neurosis Group Clinical Trials Register (CCDANCTR). We identified 31,482 citations, with an additional citation identified through other sources. Some 16,381 citations remained after deduplication of 15,102 citations. Of these, we excluded 16,169 citations and retrieved 212 full papers. We assessed these against the selection criteria and excluded a further 177 full‐text articles.

We included 35 studies (Abdelbasset 2019 \[[176](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0176 "AbdelbassetWK , AlqahtaniBA . A randomized controlled trial on the impact of moderate-intensity continuous aerobic exercise on the depression status of middle-aged patients with congestive heart failure. Medicine2019;98(17):e15344. Through the database searches, plus additional searching of Clinicaltrials.gov, the International Clinical Trials Registry Platform (ICTRP) and the ISRCTN registry in July 2021 and September 2025, we identified an additional 66 ongoing studies (see [Supplementary material 4](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0004)).

See the PRISMA flow diagram for details of the study selection process for the current update ([Figure 1](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0001)).

#### Included studies

Exercise training for depressed older adults with Alzheimer's disease. Aging & Mental Health2008;12(1):72-80. ")\]; Yu 2023). The 73 trials recruited a total of at least 4985 participants at randomisation, and at least 3624 participants completed the trials. In two trials, data were not reported on the number of participants at randomisation or follow‐up (Reuter 1984; Wang 2022), while eight trials did not report participant numbers at follow‐up (Brenes 2007; Epstein 1986; Hess‐Homeier 1981; Khorvash 2012; Khoshnab 2017; Pinchasov 2000; Reuter 1984; Wang 2022).

We contacted the authors of three studies for additional data and received one response (Schuch 2015).

See [Table 2](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-tbl-0006) and [Supplementary material 7](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0007) for details about the characteristics of the included studies.

All studies are RCTs.

Scale names and acronyms are reported as designated by the trial authors.

Abbreviations: ID: identification; NR: not reported

#### Excluded studies

In total, 326 studies have been excluded from the various versions of the review after full‐text review; the reasons for exclusion are listed below. As with previous updates, we decided not to list reviews as excluded studies. Further details of excluded studies can be found in [Supplementary material 3](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0003).

In 195 studies, participants either did not have to have depression (as defined by the authors of the study) to be eligible for the study, had a condition that was excluded (e.g. dysthymia, bipolar, postnatal depression), or were a subgroup with another condition (e.g. people with obesity) (Abascal 2008 \[[280](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0280 "AbascalL . The effect of depression and adherence in a dietary and physical activity intervention for overweight and obese adults [dissertation]. University of California, San Diego and San Diego State University2008. ")\]; Abd El‐Kadar 2016 \[[281](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0281 "Abd El-KaderSM , Al-JiffriOH . Exercise alleviates depression related systemic inflammation in chronic obstructive pulmonary disease patients. African Historical Studies2016;16(4):1078-88. ")\]; Abt 2005 \[[282](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0282 "AbtK . ##### Ongoing studies

We identified 66 ongoing studies through our searches of CCDANCTR (23 studies: Heinzel 2018 \[[623](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0623 "HeinzelS , RappMA , FydrichT , StrohleA , TeranC , KalliesG , et al. ")\]; Heissel 2020 \[[624](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0624 "HeisselA , PietrekA , SchwefelM , AbulaK , WilbertzG , HeinzelS , et al. Added to the nine studies that remain ongoing from the 2013 update by Cooney and colleagues (ACTRN12612000094875; CTR/2012/09/002985; NCT00931814; NCT01383811; NCT01464463; NCT01619930; NCT01696201; NCT01763983; UMIN000001488), there are currently 75 studies ongoing. See [Supplementary material 4](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0004).

##### New studies found for this update

We are including 35 additional trials, which recruited a total of 2526 participants (where reported) at randomisation. One trial did not state the number of participants randomised (Wang 2022). Of these, 1844 participants (where reported) remained in the trials by the time of final outcome analysis (Abdelbasset 2019; Abdollahi 2017; Bademli 2023; Barclay 2014; Belvederi Murri 2015; Bose 2015; Brush 2022; Buschert 2019; Cheung 2018; De Groot 2019; Doose 2015; Eman 2017; Haller 2018; Heinzel 2022; Heissel 2015; Hidalgo 2021; Ho 2014; Huang 2015; Karg 2020; Khorvash 2012; Khoshnab 2017; Kruisdijk 2018; La Rocque 2021; Legrand 2014; Legrand 2016; Olson 2017; Pfaff 2014; Prakhinkit 2014; Roy 2018; Salehi 2016; Schuch 2015; Siqueira 2016; Taheri 2018; Wang 2022; Yu 2023). Three trials did not provide participant numbers at follow‐up (Khorvash 2012; Khoshnab 2017; Wang 2022). See [Supplementary material 2](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0002).

### Risk of bias in included studies

#### Random sequence generation

We classified 30 trials as being at low risk of bias (Bademli 2023; Belvederi Murri 2015; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Brenes 2007; Brush 2022; Cheung 2018; De Groot 2019; Haller 2018; Heinzel 2022; Hidalgo 2021; Ho 2014; Hoffman 2010; Huang 2015; Karg 2020; Knubben 2007; Krogh 2009; La Rocque 2021; Legrand 2014; Legrand 2016; Martinsen 1985; Mather 2002; Olson 2017; Pfaff 2014; Prakhinkit 2014; Sims 2009; Singh 1997; Singh 2005; Siqueira 2016; Yu 2023), one as being at high risk of bias (Hemat‐Far 2012), and 42 trials as being at unclear risk of bias. A graphical representation of the risk of bias assessment can be seen in [Figure 2](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0002) and [Figure 3](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0003). See [Supplementary material 2](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0002) for the full risk of bias assessment for each study.
