'Risk of bias' summary: review authors' judgements about each risk of bias item for each included study

#### Allocation concealment

Allocation concealment was adequate and therefore at low risk of bias in 22 trials (Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Cheung 2018; De Groot 2019; Dunn 2005; Ho 2014; Hoffman 2010; Karg 2020; Knubben 2007; Krogh 2009; La Rocque 2021; Legrand 2016; Martinsen 1985; Mather 2002; Pfaff 2014; Sims 2009; Singh 1997; Singh 2005; Siqueira 2016; Veale 1992; Williams 2008). For the remaining trials, we judged the risk of bias as unclear or high (Abdelbasset 2019; Abdollahi 2017; Bademli 2023; Barclay 2014; Belvederi Murri 2015; Bonnet 2005; Bose 2015; Brenes 2007; Brush 2022; Buschert 2019; Chu 2008; Doose 2015; Doyne 1987; Eman 2017; Epstein 1986; Fetsch 1979; Foley 2008; Fremont 1987; Gary 2010; Greist 1979; Haller 2018; Heinzel 2022; Heissel 2015; Hemat‐Far 2012; Hess‐Homeier 1981; Hidalgo 2021; Huang 2015; Khorvash 2012; Khoshnab 2017; Klein 1985; Kruisdijk 2018; Legrand 2014; McCann 1984; McNeil 1991; Mota‐Pereira 2011; Mutrie 1988; Nabkasorn 2005; Olson 2017; Orth 1979; Pilu 2007; Pinchasov 2000; Prakhinkit 2014; Reuter 1984; Roy 2018; Salehi 2016; Schuch 2015; Setaro 1985; Shahidi 2011; Taheri 2018; Wang 2022; Yu 2023).

#### Blinding of participants

In exercise trials, participants cannot be blinded to the treatment allocation. We were advised by Cochrane Editors to classify all trials as being at high risk of bias.

#### Blinding of those delivering the intervention

Similarly, for those trials where supervised exercise was provided, the person delivering the intervention could not be blinded, so we were advised by the Cochrane Editors to classify all trials as being at high risk of bias (note that not all studies reported whether exercise was performed with or without supervision).

#### Blinding of outcome assessors

Twenty‐three trials included blinding of the outcome assessors, so we classified them as being at low risk of bias (Abdelbasset 2019; Belvederi Murri 2015; Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Bose 2015; Brenes 2007; Cheung 2018; De Groot 2019; Dunn 2005; Gary 2010; Ho 2014; Huang 2015; Karg 2020; Knubben 2007; Krogh 2009; La Rocque 2021; Mather 2002; Mota‐Pereira 2011; Pfaff 2014; Singh 2005; Williams 2008; Yu 2023). We classified 19 trials as unclear (Abdollahi 2017; Bademli 2023; Barclay 2014; Buschert 2019; Eman 2017; Haller 2018; Heinzel 2022; Heissel 2015; Hidalgo 2021; Khorvash 2012; Kruisdijk 2018; Olson 2017; Pilu 2007; Pinchasov 2000; Roy 2018; Salehi 2016; Schuch 2015; Setaro 1985; Wang 2022) and 31 trials at high risk (Bonnet 2005; Brush 2022; Chu 2008; Doose 2015; Doyne 1987; Epstein 1986; Fetsch 1979; Foley 2008; Fremont 1987; Greist 1979; Hemat‐Far 2012; Hess‐Homeier 1981; Hoffman 2010; Khoshnab 2017; Klein 1985; Legrand 2014; Legrand 2016; Martinsen 1985; McCann 1984; McNeil 1991; Mutrie 1988; Nabkasorn 2005; Orth 1979; Prakhinkit 2014; Reuter 1984; Shahidi 2011; Sims 2009; Singh 1997; Siqueira 2016; Taheri 2018; Veale 1992).

#### Incomplete outcome data

We classified 31 trials as low risk of bias because they performed ‘intention‐to‐treat’ (ITT) analysis (i.e. complete outcome data were reported) or, if there were missing outcome data, these were replaced using a recognised statistical method (e.g. last observation carried forward), and participants remained in the group to which they had been allocated (Abdelbasset 2019; Bademli 2023; Barclay 2014; Belvederi Murri 2015; Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Brush 2022; Cheung 2018; De Groot 2019; Doose 2015; Dunn 2005; Hidalgo 2021; Ho 2014; Hoffman 2010; Huang 2015; Karg 2020; Krogh 2009; La Rocque 2021; Legrand 2016; Mather 2002; McNeil 1991; Mota‐Pereira 2011; Mutrie 1988; Orth 1979; Pfaff 2014; Pilu 2007; Salehi 2016; Schuch 2015; Singh 1997; Siqueira 2016).

One trial reported data for individual participants (Orth 1979), so by using 'last observation carried forward', we replaced data from the participants who did not complete the trial and included these data in the meta‐analysis of ITT trials. One trial reported that the analysis was ITT, because it used a ‘worse‐case’ scenario assumption to replace data from participants who did not complete the trial, but they included only 38 of the 39 randomised participants in the analyses, so we classified it as ‘not ITT’ (Knubben 2007). The remaining studies were classified as being at unclear or high risk of bias. Most trials did not report data from participants who dropped out.

#### Selective reporting

We attempted to identify whether a protocol was available by screening the reference list of the publications. We identified protocols for eight trials and checked that all prespecified outcome events were reported, and rated these as being at low risk of bias (Barclay 2014; Blumenthal 2012a; Dunn 2005; Heinzel 2022; Karg 2020; Krogh 2009; La Rocque 2021; Yu 2023).

We classified 19 other trials as being at low risk of bias as we judged that there was sufficient information in the methods to be sure that the trials had reported all their planned outcomes (Belvederi Murri 2015; Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Cheung 2018; De Groot 2019; Doose 2015; Gary 2010; Hemat‐Far 2012; Hoffman 2010; Ho 2014; Huang 2015; Khoshnab 2017; Legrand 2014; Legrand 2016; Pfaff 2014; Prakhinkit 2014; Mota‐Pereira 2011; Shahidi 2011; Siqueira 2016).

We classified five trials at high risk of selective reporting bias. One trial did not have a methods section and only reported outcomes within the results (Greist 1979), while a second trial stated that there was an eight‐month interview follow‐up, but these data were not reported (Pilu 2007). A third trial did not report any measure of variation for all its outcome measures (Khorvash 2012). One trial did not report outcomes at follow‐up as was indicated it would do in the protocol (Hidalgo 2021). One trial reported data only for the intervention before and after its use rather than in comparison to the control intervention (Haller 2018).

#### Other biases

We considered seven trials to be at low risk of bias in terms of other potential biases (Bademli 2023; Brush 2022; Cheung 2018; Gary 2010; Karg 2020; Mota‐Pereira 2011; Siqueira 2016), with six trials at high risk of bias (Greist 1979; Haller 2018; Hemat‐Far 2012; Kruisdijk 2018; Taheri 2018; Wang 2022).

For 60 trials, the risk of bias was unclear (Abdelbasset 2019; Abdollahi 2017; Barclay 2014; Belvederi Murri 2015; Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Bonnet 2005; Bose 2015; Brenes 2007; Buschert 2019; Chu 2008; De Groot 2019; Doose 2015; Doyne 1987; Dunn 2005; Eman 2017; Epstein 1986; Fetsch 1979; Foley 2008; Fremont 1987; Heinzel 2022; Heissel 2015; Hess‐Homeier 1981; Hidalgo 2021; Ho 2014; Hoffman 2010; Huang 2015; Khorvash 2012; Khoshnab 2017; Klein 1985; Knubben 2007; Krogh 2009; La Rocque 2021; Legrand 2014; Legrand 2016; Martinsen 1985; Mather 2002; McCann 1984; McNeil 1991; Mutrie 1988; Nabkasorn 2005; Olson 2017; Orth 1979; Pfaff 2014; Pilu 2007; Pinchasov 2000; Prakhinkit 2014; Reuter 1984; Roy 2018; Salehi 2016; Schuch 2015; Setaro 1985; Shahidi 2011; Sims 2009; Singh 1997; Singh 2005; Veale 1992; Williams 2008; Yu 2023).

On the basis of the first 50 participants, one study changed the sample size calculation based on the observed standard deviation (Krogh 2009; their initial calculation was that a minimum of 186 participants (standard deviation (SD) = 6) were needed, but for the first 50 participants, the SD was 3.9, so they adjusted their sample size calculation to 135 participants). Hemat‐Far 2012 told the control group to reduce their activity.

We decided to include data on continuous depression scores in our meta‐analysis, rather than depression measured as a dichotomous outcome. This is because we knew from previous updates that very few trials had reported depression as a dichotomous outcome, and we wished to include as many trials as possible in our meta‐analysis. For future updates, we will consider whether to perform a separate meta‐analysis for trials that measured depression as a dichotomous outcome.

#### Publication bias

Visually, our funnel plot appeared to suggest some missing studies that may be due to a degree of publication bias, outcome reporting bias or heterogeneity (Egger regression intercept P value was 0.00265) \[[689](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0689 "EggerM , Davey SmithG , MinderC . Bias in meta-analysis detected by a simple, graphical test. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)1997;315(7109):629-34.")\] (funnel plot for exercise versus control ‐ analysis 1.1 ([Figure 4](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0004))). Use of Duval and Tweedie's trim and fill suggests that 15 studies may have been trimmed from the right of the mean (i.e. small studies favouring control intervention), and the adjusted SMD was −0.45 (95% CI −0.62 to −0.28), representing a moderate benefit from exercise \[[690](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0690 "DuvalS , TweedieR . Trim and fill: a simple funnel-plot-based method of testing and adjusting for publication bias in meta-analysis. Biometrics2000;56(2):455-63.")\].

Figure 4

---

![Funnel plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus control, outcome: 1.1 Reduction in depression symptoms post‐treatment](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/media/CDSR/CD004366/image_n/nCD004366-FIG-05.svg)

---

Funnel plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus control, outcome: 1.1 Reduction in depression symptoms post‐treatment

### Synthesis of results

#### Comparison I: exercise versus control

Sixty‐one trials (2576 participants) included a comparison of exercise with a control intervention, although only 57 trials (2189 participants) reported data that could be meta‐analysed (see [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

##### Primary outcome measure

###### 1.1 Reduction in depressive symptom severity

**Post‐treatment**

The pooled standardised mean difference (SMD) for the 57 trials (2189 participants), which was calculated using a random‐effects model, was −0.67 (95% CI −0.82 to −0.52; I² = 61, P < 0.001; GRADE: low), showing that exercise may result in a reduction in depressive symptoms (Analysis 1.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005) and [Figure 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0005)).

Figure 5

---

![Forest plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus 'control', outcome 1.1 Reduction in depression symptoms post‐treatment](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/media/CDSR/CD004366/image_n/nCD004366-FIG-04.svg)

---

Forest plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus 'control', outcome 1.1 Reduction in depression symptoms post‐treatment

**End of long‐term follow‐up**

We were able to pool nine trials (405 participants) that provided long‐term follow‐up data, ranging from 8 weeks to 12 months of follow‐up (Blumenthal 1999; Fremont 1987; Gary 2010; Heinzel 2022; Huang 2015; Klein 1985; Krogh 2009; Sims 2009; Wang 2022). The pooled SMD (random‐effects) showed that the evidence is very uncertain about the effect of exercise on depressive symptoms (SMD −0.53, 95% CI −1.11 to 0.06; I² = 87, P < 0.001; GRADE: very low) (Analysis 1.2; [Figure 6](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0006)). Follow‐up data from Blumenthal 2007 were reported according to the proportion who had fully or partially remitted from depression, but continuous mood scores were not reported, so we could not include these data in the meta‐analysis.

Figure 6

---

![Forest plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus 'control', outcome: 1.2 Reduction in depression symptoms follow‐up](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/media/CDSR/CD004366/image_n/nCD004366-FIG-07.svg)

---

Forest plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus 'control', outcome: 1.2 Reduction in depression symptoms follow‐up

##### Secondary outcome measures

###### 1.2a Acceptability of treatment: attendance at exercise

Twenty‐six trials reported attendance rates for exercise. These were 50.6% for aerobic and 56.2% for strength exercise groups (Krogh 2009), 58% (Doose 2015; Pfaff 2014), 66.9% for exercise and 68.8% for yoga (La Rocque 2021), 68% (Legrand 2014), 70% (Doyne 1987), 72% (Dunn 2005), 73% (Ho 2014), 75% for progressive aerobic plus sertraline and 71% for non‐progressive exercise plus sertraline (Belvederi Murri 2015), 78% (Nabkasorn 2005), 82% (Gary 2010), 84% for endurance and 90% for strength exercise (Haller 2018), 91% (Mota‐Pereira 2011; Schuch 2015), 92% (Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007), 92.7% for moderate and 95% for vigorous exercise (Yu 2023), 92.9% and 81.8% attended ≥ 8 sessions, respectively (Legrand 2016), 94% (Blumenthal 2012a), 97.3% for high‐intensity and 99.1% for low‐intensity (Chu 2008), 95% to 100% (Singh 2005) and 100% (Huang 2015). One trial reported the mean number of exercise sessions attended as 5.88 (Hoffman 2010). Another trial reported that 1.71 and 1.60 exercise sessions were attended for high‐ and low‐intensity exercise, respectively (Heinzel 2022). One trial rescheduled missed visits (McNeil 1991) so participants attended the full course of exercise. One trial reported high adherence (no data reported) (Heissel 2015). As with exercise intensity, it is difficult to attribute any differences in outcome to differences in attendance rates because there were other sources of variation in the type of interventions (e.g. duration of intervention, type of exercise) and differences in the risk of bias between trials that might account for differences in outcome.

###### 1.2b Acceptability of treatment: completing the intervention or control

We extracted data on the number of participants who were randomised in each trial and the number who completed each trial ([Table 1](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-tbl-0005)). This ranged from 100% completion (Bademli 2023; Barclay 2014; Hemat‐Far 2012; Huang 2015; Khoshnab 2017; McNeil 1991; Mutrie 1988; Orth 1979; Pilu 2007; Salehi 2016; Siqueira 2016; Taheri 2018) to 46% completion (Hidalgo 2021). For the exercise intervention, this ranged from 100% completion (Bademli 2023; Barclay 2014; Cheung 2018; Doose 2015; Gary 2010; Hemat‐Far 2012; Huang 2015; Khoshnab 2017; McNeil 1991; Mutrie 1988; Orth 1979; Pilu 2007; Salehi 2016; Siqueira 2016; Taheri 2018) to 38% completion (Hidalgo 2021). Fifty‐one studies (2311 participants) reported how many participants completed the exercise and control arms (Analysis 1.3; [Figure 7](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0007)), producing a risk ratio of (RR) 1.0 (95% CI 0.98 to 1.02; GRADE: moderate certainty), showing that exercise results in little or no difference in completion compared to control.

Figure 7

---

![Funnel plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus 'control', outcome: 1.3 Completed intervention or control](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/media/CDSR/CD004366/image_n/nCD004366-FIG-08.svg)

---

Funnel plot of comparison: 1 Exercise versus 'control', outcome: 1.3 Completed intervention or control

###### 1.3 Quality of life

Seven studies reported measures of quality of life at the end of the study (Brenes 2007; Gary 2010; Huang 2015; Pilu 2007; Schuch 2015; Sims 2009; Singh 2005), comparing interventions on different domains of quality‐of‐life questionnaires (Analysis 1.4 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). One trial reported changes in quality of life without presenting the necessary data to include in the meta‐analysis (Singh 2005), and another reported quality of life at baseline but not at follow‐up (Sims 2009).

The evidence was very uncertain for the quality of life mental health domain (SMD −0.05, 95% CI −0.85 to 0.76; I² = 74%, P = 0.02; 3 studies, 98 participants; GRADE: very low) and psychological domain (SMD 0.35, 95% CI −1.22 to 1.92; I² = 90%, P = 0.001; 2 studies, 80 participants; GRADE: very low). For social domains, exercise may result in little to no difference following exercise compared to control (SMD 0.37, 95% CI −0.18 to 0.92; I² = 29%, P = 0.24; 2 studies, 80 participants; GRADE: low). In contrast, exercise may result in an increase in the environment domain (SMD 0.51, 95% CI 0.05 to 0.96; I² = 0%, P = 0.82; 2 studies, 80 participants; GRADE: low) and physical domain (SMD 0.57, 95% CI 0.15 to 0.98; I² = 44%, P = 0.13; 5 studies, 178 participants; GRADE: very low) of quality of life, although the evidence is very uncertain for the physical domain. One study (39 participants) reported on a quality of life domain, where the evidence on the effect of exercise on quality of life was very uncertain (SMD 0.56, 95% CI −0.08 to 1.20; GRADE: very low).

**1.4 Cost**

No trial reported the cost or cost‐effectiveness of exercise compared to another intervention.

###### 1.5 Adverse events

Eleven trials reported data on adverse events between the exercise and the control groups (Belvederi Murri 2015; Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Doose 2015; Dunn 2005; Knubben 2007; Legrand 2016; Prakhinkit 2014; Sims 2009; Singh 2005), showing that exercise is likely to result in little to no difference in adverse events compared to control. Dunn 2005 reported increased severity of depressive symptoms (n = 1), chest pain (n = 1) and joint pain/swelling (n = 1); all of these participants discontinued exercise. Singh 2005 reported adverse events in detail (visits to a health professional, minor illness, muscular pain, chest pain, injuries requiring training adjustment, falls, deaths and hospital days) and found no difference between the groups. Knubben 2007 reported “no negative effects of exercise (muscle pain, tightness or fatigue)” in the course of the programme; two weeks after the training had finished, one person in the placebo group required gastric lavage and one person in the exercise group inflicted a superficial cut in her arm. Sims 2009 reported no adverse events or falls in either the exercise or control groups. Blumenthal 1999 reported that five participants in the exercise plus sertraline group dropped out due to adverse events associated with sertraline and four participants due to musculoskeletal injury. Five participants from the sertraline‐only group dropped out owing to medication adverse events (Blumenthal 1999). Blumenthal 2007 reported side effects using a participant rating of a 36‐item checklist, with the only statistically significant difference (P = 0.03) reported for participants experiencing post‐treatment diarrhoea and loose stools (sertraline 31%; home‐based exercise 21%; supervised exercise 10%; placebo 12%). Blumenthal 2012a reported more fatigue and sexual dysfunction in the sertraline group (20% and 26%, respectively) than in the exercise group (2.4% for both side effects). Although Prakhinkit 2014 reported that five participants withdrew either due to loss to follow‐up, physical discomfort or lack of compliance, no details were provided regarding which interventions they received. Belvederi Murri 2015 reported two participants engaged with physical exercise plus sertraline left the study, one due to having ankle distortion and another through worsening musculoskeletal pain. Doose 2015 found that seven participants in the exercise group reported adverse events, including three who were unable to continue due to worsening of pre‐existing orthopaedic injuries, two who were admitted to psychiatric wards with major depression and two who withdrew for personal reasons. Four in the control group also reported adverse events: one was admitted to hospital, one felt physically incapable of participating, one did not want to participate due to worsened pre‐existing chronic neck pain and one withdrew for personal reasons (Doose 2015). Adverse events were reported in the exercise intervention group by Legrand 2016, including transient muscular/joint soreness (n = 3), headaches (n = 1) and fatigue (n = 2). Due to the diversity of different adverse events reported, we decided not to meta‐analyse these data.

#### Comparison 2: exercise versus psychological therapies

Eleven trials (918 participants) compared exercise with psychological therapies (De Groot 2019; Epstein 1986; Fetsch 1979; Fremont 1987; Gary 2010; Greist 1979; Hess‐Homeier 1981; Huang 2015; Karg 2020; Klein 1985; Setaro 1985).

##### Primary outcome

###### 2.1 Reduction in depressive symptom severity

**Post‐treatment**

We included data from 10 trials (414 participants) comparing exercise with psychological therapies in a meta‐analysis (De Groot 2019; Epstein 1986; Fetsch 1979; Fremont 1987; Gary 2010; Hess‐Homeier 1981; Huang 2015; Karg 2020; Klein 1985; Setaro 1985). When data were pooled, the SMD was 0.03 (95% CI −0.16 to 0.23), showing that exercise results in little to no difference compared to psychological therapies in changing depressive symptoms (Analysis 2.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). The analysis provides moderate‐certainty evidence, with heterogeneity that was considered unimportant (I² = 0%, P = 0.85).

**End of long‐term follow‐up**

Long‐term follow‐up was reported by four studies (114 participants) ranging from 4 to 9 months (Fremont 1987; Gary 2010; Huang 2015; Klein 1985), which we judged as low‐certainty evidence. When pooled in a random‐effects meta‐analysis (Analysis 2.2 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), the SMD of −0.11 (95% CI ‐0.48 to 0.26) showed that exercise may result in little to no difference compared to psychological therapies in changing depressive symptoms. No statistical heterogeneity was found (I² = 0%, P = 0.47).

##### Secondary outcomes

###### 2.2a Acceptability of treatment: attendance at exercise sessions

Two trials reported attendance rates (Gary 2010; Huang 2015). One trial reported adherence scores that were calculated based on the number of sessions attended of those prescribed. This trial reported that the adherence rates were 82% for exercise and 72% for CBT (Gary 2010). A second trial reported that the exercise group achieved 100% at 3‐month follow‐up, declining to 47% at 6‐ and 9‐month follow‐up (Huang 2015). Adherence rates for CBT were not reported (Huang 2015).

###### 2.2b Acceptability of treatment: completing the intervention or psychological therapies

Seven trials (433 participants) reported the number of participants completing either the intervention trial or psychological therapies (Analysis 2.3 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)) (De Groot 2019; Fetsch 1979; Fremont 1987; Gary 2010; Huang 2015; Karg 2020; Klein 1985). The risk ratio for treatment acceptability was 1.06 (95% CI 0.99 to 1.13; GRADE: moderate), showing that exercise probably results in little to no difference in completion of the intervention compared to psychological therapies.

###### 2.3. Quality of life

Two trials (72 participants) reported changes in quality of life measures, specifically the Minnesota Living with Heart Failure Questionnaire (Gary 2010) and the SF‐36 (Huang 2015) (Analysis 2.4 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

When pooled through meta‐analysis, it was shown that exercise may result in a slight increase in the physical domain of quality of life compared to psychological therapy (SMD 0.25, 95% CI −0.21 to 0.72; GRADE: low). In contrast, there was little to no difference between exercise and psychological therapies for the mental domain (SMD 0.05, 95% CI −0.41 to 0.52; GRADE: low). No statistical heterogeneity was evident for either physical (I² = 0%, P = 0.32) or mental (I² = 0%, P = 0.81) domains. Only one trial (37 participants) reported a summary quality of life score (Huang 2015), finding that exercise may result in a slight increase in quality of life compared to psychological therapy, although the evidence was very uncertain (SMD 0.37, 95% CI −0.28 to 1.02; GRADE: very low).

###### 2.4 Cost

No trial reported the cost or cost‐effectiveness of exercise compared to another intervention.

###### 2.5 Adverse events

One trial by Karg 2020 reported that eight participants dropped out of the exercise intervention group, with one participant reporting physical complaints. Although 12 participants dropped out of the comparator group (CBT), none were identified as an adverse event associated with the comparator intervention (versus CBT).

#### Comparison 3: exercise versus pharmacological treatments

Six trials (677 participants) assessed the effectiveness of exercise compared to pharmacological treatments (Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Brenes 2007; Hidalgo 2021; Khoshnab 2017).

##### Primary outcome

###### 3.1 Reduction in depressive symptom severity

**Post‐treatment**

For the five trials (330 participants) that compared exercise with pharmacological treatments (Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Brenes 2007; Khoshnab 2017) the SMD was −0.11 (95% CI −0.33 to 0.10) (Analysis 3.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), indicating that exercise results in little to difference in depressive symptoms compared to pharmacological treatments. No statistical heterogeneity was indicated (I² = 0%, P = 0.69).

**End of long‐term follow‐up**

One study (58 participants) reported long‐term follow‐up at 6 months (Blumenthal 1999), suggesting that exercise may result in a slight reduction in depressive symptoms compared to pharmacological treatment, although the evidence is very uncertain (MD ‐0.40, 95% CI −0.80 to 0.00) (Analysis 3.2 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

##### Secondary outcomes

###### 3.2a Acceptability of treatment: attendance at exercise

Four trials reported on attendance rates (Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Hidalgo 2021). Three trials reported attendance rates as 82% (Blumenthal 2007), 89.6% (Blumenthal 1999) and 94% (Blumenthal 2012a) for participants receiving exercise alone. Adherence to pharmacological treatment differed among the trials, ranging from 83% (Blumenthal 2007) to 100% achieving acceptable adherence (Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2012a). A fourth trial reported that the mean number of sessions of physical activity attended was 18.9 (Hidalgo 2021).

###### 3.2b Acceptability of treatment: completing the intervention or pharmacological intervention

For participant completion of the trial, there were data from five trials (655 participants) (Blumenthal 1999; Blumenthal 2007; Blumenthal 2012a; Hidalgo 2021; Khoshnab 2017). The risk ratio was 0.92 (95% CI 0.77 to 1.10; GRADE: low) (Analysis 3.3 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

###### 3.3. Quality of life

One trial (25 participants) by Brenes 2007 showed that the evidence is very uncertain about the benefits of exercise compared to pharmacological treatments on the SF‐36 mental health domain (MD ‐11.90, 95% CI −24.04 to 0.24; GRADE: very low) and physical health domain (MD 1.30, 95% CI −0.67 to 3.27; GRADE: very low) (Analysis 3.4 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

###### 3.4 Cost

No trial reported the cost or cost‐effectiveness of exercise compared to another intervention.

###### 3.5 Adverse events

Blumenthal 1999 reported that three of 53 participants in the exercise group suffered musculoskeletal injuries; five participants from the medication group experienced medication adverse events. Blumenthal 2007 collected data on side effects by asking participants to rate a 36‐item somatic symptom checklist and reported that “a few patients reported worsening of symptoms”. Of the 36 side effects assessed, only one showed a statistically significant group difference (P = 0.03), with the sertraline group reporting worse post‐treatment diarrhoea and loose stools (31%) compared to home‐based exercise (21%) and supervised exercise (10%). Blumenthal 2012a also assessed the 36 side effects following the approach by Blumenthal 2007. Only two side effects differed significantly between the groups, with the sertraline group having worse post‐treatment fatigue (20%) and increased sexual problems (26%) than the exercise group (2.4% for both side effects). Hidalgo 2021 reported that 8.9% of people undertaking the exercise intervention reported adverse events, including osteomuscular pain, mild contusions or dizziness. In the comparator group receiving antidepressant therapy, 22.5% reported adverse events, including dizziness (6.5%), epigastric pain (5.2%) or daytime drowsiness (3.2%) (Hidalgo 2021). The evidence indicates that exercise may result in fewer adverse events than pharmacological treatments.

#### Comparison 4: exercise versus 'other' comparators

Nine studies (365 participants) compared exercise with one of five other comparator interventions, which were bright light therapy (Pinchasov 2000), reflexology (Eman 2017), occupational or art therapy (Buschert 2019; Martinsen 1985), supplements (Taheri 2018) and health education (Abdelbasset 2019; Bose 2015; Mather 2002; Singh 1997).

##### Primary outcome

###### 4.1 Reduction in depressive symptom severity

**Post‐treatment**

Separate studies comparing exercise to bright light therapy (SMD −1.48, 95% CI −2.56 to −0.41; 18 participants) (Pinchasov 2000), reflexology (SMD −1.32, 95% CI −2.01 to −0.63; 40 participants) (Eman 2017) and supplements (SMD −1.41, 95% CI −2.54 to −0.28; 16 participants) (Taheri 2018) found that exercise may be more effective than the different comparators (Analysis 4.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). Also, exercise may be more effective than health education (SMD −1.16, 95% CI −1.99 to −0.33; 218 participants), although there was considerable statistical heterogeneity (I² = 87%, P < 0.001) (Abdelbasset 2019; Bose 2015; Mather 2002; Singh 1997). For the two trials (73 participants) that compared exercise with occupational or art therapy (Buschert 2019; Martinsen 1985), the SMD was −0.51 (95% CI −1.75 to 0.73), with considerable statistical heterogeneity (I² = 85%, P = 0.01), indicating that there may be little difference between the two interventions. In all comparisons, the limited evidence makes the findings very uncertain.

**End of long‐term follow‐up**

Three studies (170 participants) reported long‐term follow‐up for the comparison of exercise with health education ranging from 8 weeks to 26 months (Bose 2015; Mather 2002; Singh 1997), reporting that exercise may provide a comparative benefit (SMD −1.22, 95% CI −2.63 to 0.19; I² = 93%, P < 0.001) (Analysis 4.2 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). No studies reported long‐term follow‐up in comparisons with bright light therapy, reflexology, occupational or art therapy, or supplements.

##### Secondary outcomes

###### 4.2a Acceptability of treatment: attendance at exercise

None of the trials of bright light therapy, reflexology, occupational or art therapy, or supplements reported this outcome. Two of the four trials assessing health education reported attendance rates, varying from 59% for aerobic exercise (Mather 2002) to 93% for high‐intensity exercise (Singh 1997).

###### 4.2b Acceptability of treatment: completing the intervention or control

All participants in the trials comparing exercise with reflexology (Eman 2017) and with supplements (Taheri 2018) completed the trial. The trial of bright light therapy did not report on this outcome (Pinchasov 2000). Two trials of occupational or art therapy reported on the acceptability of treatment (Buschert 2019; Martinsen 1985), with a risk ratio of 0.99 (95% CI 0.82 to 1.20) (Analysis 4.3 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). All four trials comparing exercise with health education reported the number of participants randomised and completing the trial (Abdelbasset 2019; Bose 2015; Mather 2002; Singh 1997), reporting a risk ratio of 1.01 (95% CI 0.96 to 1.05) (Analysis 4.3 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

###### 4.3. Quality of life

None of the trials reported on this outcome.

###### 4.4 Cost

No trial reported the cost or cost‐effectiveness of exercise compared to another intervention.

###### 4.5 Adverse events

Only the trial by Singh 1997 comparing exercise with health education reported on adverse events, with one participant undertaking exercise being referred to a psychologist at six weeks due to increasing suicidality, and musculoskeletal symptoms in two participants necessitated adjustment of the training regime.

#### Subgroup analysis for reduction in depressive symptom severity

##### Type of exercise

We explored the influence of the type of exercise (aerobic, mixed or resistance) on outcomes for those trials comparing exercise versus control (Analysis 5.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). All types of exercise appeared beneficial compared to control, with no evidence of a difference between the subgroups (P = 0.60). Forty‐eight studies (1937 participants) assessing aerobic exercise found a moderate to large benefit from exercise (SMD −0.64, 95% CI −0.80 to −0.48), although there was substantial heterogeneity (I² = 62%, P < 0.001). Mixed exercise was assessed by six studies (139 participants), which when pooled showed a large clinical effect from exercise compared to control (SMD −0.92, 95% CI −1.66 to −0.17), with substantial heterogeneity (I² = 73%, P = 0.002). The three studies (112 participants) of resistance exercise appear to show a comparative benefit from exercise (SMD −0.80, 95% CI −1.20 to −0.41), although confidence intervals were fairly wide. The effects of heterogeneity were considered not to be important (I² = 0%, P = 0.48).

##### Intensity and sessions of exercise

We explored the influence of intensity of exercise (very light, light, moderate, vigorous, near maximum to maximum) on the reduction of depression for those trials comparing exercise versus control (Analysis 5.2 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), finding that there was no difference between the subgroups (P = 0.72). Fifty‐seven studies making 59 different comparisons at four different levels of intensity (i.e. very light, light, moderate, vigorous), were included in the analyses. One study employed very light intensity exercise, finding limited difference between exercise and control (SMD −0.31, 95% CI −1.13 to 0.50). Eight studies (357 participants) carried out light‐intensity exercise, which had a moderate clinical effect (SMD −0.66, 95% CI −0.94 to −0.38), with heterogeneity we considered to be moderate (I² = 32%, P = 0.17). Thirty‐one studies (1020 participants) carried out moderate‐intensity exercise resulting in a moderate to large clinical effect (SMD −0.71, 95% CI −0.92 to −0.49); however, the analysis was affected by moderate to substantial heterogeneity (I² = 59%, P < 0.001). In the 19 studies (819 participants) that carried out vigorous intensity exercise, there was a moderate effect (SMD −0.57, 95% CI −0.82 to −0.31), with substantial heterogeneity (I² = 65%, P < 0.001).

We explored the influence of the total number of prescribed exercise sessions (0 to 12, 13 to 24, 25 to 36, 37+ sessions) on the reduction in depressive symptoms for those trials comparing exercise versus control (Analysis 5.3 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). Although exercise provided greater benefit compared to the control interventions, the extent of the difference in effect did not vary significantly between the subgroups (P = 0.32). Ten studies (373 participants) providing up to 12 sessions of exercise found a moderate beneficial effect compared to control (SMD −0.52, 95% CI −1.09 to 0.04), with the meta‐analysis affected by substantial statistically significant heterogeneity (I² = 84%, P < 0.001). Increasing the number of sessions to between 13 and 36 sessions increased the benefit from exercise when compared to control. In the 20 studies (741 participants) where participants received 13 to 24 sessions (SMD −0.76, 95% CI −0.96 to −0.56) and the 11 studies (363 participants) where participants received 25 to 36 sessions (SMD −0.72, 95% CI −1.14 to −0.30), there appeared to be large benefits from exercise compared to control. In both cases, the meta‐analyses were affected by heterogeneity, although this was moderate for 13 to 24 sessions (I² =39%, P = 0.04) and substantial for 25 to 36 sessions (I² = 72%, P < 0.001). People receiving 37 or more sessions were assessed in 12 studies (559 participants), and a moderate benefit from exercise compared to control was found (SMD −0.48, 95% CI −0.68 to −0.27), with levels of heterogeneity that we considered unimportant (I² = 25%, P = 0.20). In four studies (152 participants), the number of sessions provided was unclear. When these four studies were pooled, benefit was found from exercise compared to control (SMD −0.77, 95% CI −1.11 to −0.44), with unimportant levels of heterogeneity (I² = 0%, P = 0.88).

##### Type of diagnosis

We performed subgroup analyses according to how the diagnosis of depression was made, whether it was a clinical diagnosis or categorised according to cut points on a scale or where it was unclear (Analysis 5.4 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). We found that there was no difference between the subgroups (P = 0.72). In the meta‐analysis of the 35 trials (1449 participants) where depression was diagnosed clinically, a moderate‐to‐large benefit was found for exercise versus control (SMD −0.63, 95% CI −0.81 to −0.44), although the analysis was affected by substantial heterogeneity (I² = 62%, P ˂ 0.001). Where trials used cut‐points on a scale (18 trials, 616 participants), the pooled effect showed a moderate‐to‐large benefit favouring exercise relative to control (SMD −0.73, 95% CI −0.97 to −0.49); the meta‐analysis was characterised by moderate heterogeneity (I² = 48%, P = 0.01). The method of diagnosis was unclear in four trials (123 participants). When pooled, the four trials showed uncertain effect (SMD −0.88, 95% CI −1.88 to 0.12), with the analysis affected by considerable heterogeneity (I² = 84%, P < 0.001).

##### Type of control

We categorised the type of control as either: placebo; no treatment, waiting list, usual care or self‐monitoring; exercise plus treatment versus treatment alone; stretching, meditation or relaxation; or, social/casual conversation (Analysis 5.5 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). In the previous update, the categorisations were made by one author (KD) on the basis of data extracted at the initial stage of data extraction, and were checked by a second author (GM). In the current update, all the additional studies were classified by JH, AC, DM, CH, CS, CL. Although exercise was beneficial in reducing depressive symptoms compared to each of the different types of control, the difference between the subgroups was not significant (P = 0.13). When compared with the 'no treatment, waiting list, usual care or self‐monitoring' comparator in 27 studies (897 participants), it was shown that exercise may result in a large reduction in depressive symptoms (SMD −0.85, 95% CI −1.06 to −0.64; GRADE: low), with the meta‐analysis affected by heterogeneity that we considered moderate (I² = 51%, P = 0.001). The meta‐analyses of 19 studies (812 participants) comparing exercise plus treatment versus treatment only (SMD −0.57, 95% CI −0.83 to −0.30; GRADE: very low) and the 11 studies (385 participants) comparing exercise with stretching, meditation or relaxation (SMD −0.48, 95% CI −0.80 to −0.15; GRADE: very low) found that exercise may result in a reduction in depressive symptoms, but the evidence is very uncertain. In both instances, the meta‐analyses were affected by moderate to substantial heterogeneity (exercise plus treatment with treatment only I² = 68%, P < 0.001; exercise with stretching, meditation or relaxation I² = 54%, P = 0.02). Comparison of exercise with 'social/casual conversation' in one study (29 participants) (SMD −0.48, 95% CI −1.23 to 0.27; GRADE: very low) and with 'placebo' in three studies (172 participants) (SMD −0.21, 95% CI −0.81 to 0.40; GRADE: very low) showed that exercise may result in a reduction in depressive symptoms, but the evidence is very uncertain, with the comparison with placebo affected by substantial heterogeneity (I² = 66%, P = 0.05). We considered whether to perform additional subgroup analyses according to supervised versus unsupervised exercise, indoor versus outdoor exercise and individual versus group exercise, and according to the type of control group (in the no‐treatment comparison), but we wanted to focus on the main subgroups of interest. Multiple subgroup analyses are generally considered inadvisable.

#### Sensitivity analysis

We conducted sensitivity analyses to explore the impact of study quality factors on the pooled effect size, comparing findings with the outcome from the meta‐analysis of all 57 studies (Analysis 1.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). The characteristics of interest included the type of publication, allocation concealment, use of intention‐to‐treat analysis and blinding of outcomes assessment.

##### Peer‐reviewed journal publications

For the 56 trials (2169 participants) that were reported in peer‐reviewed journal publications or doctoral theses, the SMD was −0.65 (95% CI −0.80 to −0.51), showing a moderate clinical effect in favour of exercise, with substantial heterogeneity (I² = 60%, P < 0.001) (Analysis 6.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

##### Published as abstracts/conference proceedings only

Only one study (18 participants) was published as a conference abstract. It showed benefit from exercise (MD −13.46, 95% CI −19.37 to −7.55) (Analysis 6.2 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)).

##### Allocation concealment

For the 17 trials (916 participants) with adequate allocation concealment and therefore at low risk of bias, the SMD was −0.51 (95% CI −0.72 to −0.30) (Analysis 6.3 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), showing a moderate clinical effect in favour of exercise. Although this did not show a large difference from the pooled SMD for all 57 trials of exercise versus control (SMD −0.67, 95% CI −0.82 to −0.52) (Analysis 1.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), it was lower. The meta‐analysis of the 17 trials with adequate concealment was affected by substantial heterogeneity (I² = 57%, P = 0.002).

##### Use of intention‐to‐treat analysis

For the 21 trials (994 participants) undertaking an intention‐to‐treat analysis, the pooled SMD of −0.77 (95% CI −1.05 to −0.50) showed some benefit in favour of exercise (Analysis 6.4 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), which was larger than that shown in the analysis of all 57 trials (Analysis 1.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). Again, the meta‐analysis was affected by substantial heterogeneity (I² = 74%, P < 0.001).

##### Blinded outcome assessment

For the 21 trials (998 participants) with blinded outcome assessments, the SMD was −0.53 (95% CI −0.73 to −0.33) (Analysis 6.5 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), showing a moderate effect in favour of exercise. The SMD was smaller than the pooled SMD for all 57 trials. It was affected by a substantial level of heterogeneity (I² = 54%, P = 0.002).

##### Allocation concealment, intention‐to‐treat analysis and blinded outcome assessment

For the seven trials (447 participants) with adequate allocation concealment and intention‐to‐treat analysis and blinded outcome assessment, and therefore at low risk of bias, the SMD was −0.46 (95% CI −0.88 to −0.04) (Analysis 6.6 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)), with substantial heterogeneity (I² = 78%, P < 0.001). This moderate effect in favour of exercise was smaller than that shown in the pooled SMD for all 57 trials (Analysis 1.1 in [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005): SMD −0.67, 95% CI −0.82 to −0.52).

##### Lowest dose

We assessed the effects of the dose of exercise on the outcome of reduction in depressive symptoms. Where trials included multiple arms with different doses, we included the arm with the lowest dose of exercise.

Thirteen trials included more than one dose of exercise (Belvederi Murri 2015; Blumenthal 2007; Chu 2008; Doyne 1987; Dunn 2005; Heinzel 2022; Krogh 2009; Mutrie 1988; Orth 1979; Setaro 1985; Singh 2005; Williams 2008; Yu 2023), with the largest dose replaced with the lowest dose for this sensitivity analysis (Analysis 6.7; [Figure 8](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0008); [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005)). The effect was to slightly reduce the SMD from −0.67 (95% CI −0.82 to −0.52) to −0.60 (95% CI −0.74 to −0.46), showing a similar moderate benefit from exercise. The analysis was affected by moderate heterogeneity (I² = 55%, P < 0.001). It is likely that this reflects the limited number of studies in which a lower dose was compared, as well as the difference in the type of exercise and extent of difference in the dose.

##### Recruitment and retention of participants

[Table 1](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-tbl-0005) presents data about the feasibility of recruiting and retaining participants both in the trial as a whole and in the exercise intervention in particular. We extracted data, when available, about the number of participants who were considered for inclusion in each trial, although this information was not available for all trials. The trials that did provide these data used different recruitment techniques (ranging from screening of people responding to advertisements to inclusion of those patients who were considered eligible by a referring doctor).

#### Equity assessment

We did not undertake a formal equity assessment.

#### Reporting biases

A degree of publication bias was thought to be evident from a visual inspection of the funnel plot (Egger regression intercept P = 0.00365) \[[689](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0689 "EggerM , Davey SmithG , MinderC . Bias in meta-analysis detected by a simple, graphical test. BMJ (Clinical Research Ed.)1997;315(7109):629-34.")\] (funnel plot for Analysis 1.1 ‐ exercise versus control ([Figure 4](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-fig-0004); [Supplementary material 5](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0005))). A comparison of the estimates from the meta‐analysis in Analysis 1.1 (SMD −0.67 (95% CI −0.82 to −0.52) with that from using Duval and Tweedie's trim and fill (SMD −0.45, 95% CI −0.62 to −0.28) indicates that small studies favouring control intervention may be missing, and, when adjusted, reduces the benefit from exercise \[[690](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-bib-0690 "DuvalS , TweedieR . Trim and fill: a simple funnel-plot-based method of testing and adjusting for publication bias in meta-analysis. Biometrics2000;56(2):455-63.")\]. Data for the review can be found in [Supplementary material 6](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0006).

### Data, code and other materials

As part of the published Cochrane review, the following are made available for download for users of the Cochrane Library (see [Supplementary material 6](https://www.cochranelibrary.com/cdsr/doi/10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub7/#CD004366-supitem-0006)): full search strategies for each database; full citations of each unique report for all studies (included, ongoing or awaiting classification, or excluded at the full text screen) in the final review; study data, including study information, study arms, and study results or test data; consensus risk of bias assessments; and analysis data, including overall estimates and settings, subgroup estimates, and individual data rows. Appropriate permissions have been obtained for such use. Analyses and data management were conducted within Cochrane’s authoring tool, RevMan, using the inbuilt computation methods.

colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p>Adverse events</p></td><td align="" colspan="2" rowspan="1"><p>Due to the diversity of different adverse events reported, a meta‐analysis was not performed. Adverse events were reported in both the exercise and control groups, but the nature and frequency of events varied across studies. Adverse events within the intervention group affected a relatively small number of participants, mainly related to muscle and joint problems or worsening the severity of depression. Adverse events within the control group affected a relatively small number of participants and included the need for gastric lavage, gastrointestinal symptoms such as diarrhoea and loose stools, hospital admission, physical incapacity, worsening of pre‐existing chronic neck pain and withdrawal for personal reasons.</p></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p>551<br>(10 RCTs)</p></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p>⊕⊕⊕⊝<br>Moderate <sup>a</sup></p></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p>Exercise likely results in little to no difference in adverse events compared to ‘control’.</p></td></tr><tr><td align="" colspan="7" rowspan="1"><p>* <b>The risk in the intervention group</b> (and its 95% confidence interval) is based on the assumed risk in the comparison group and the <b>relative effect</b> of the intervention (and its 95% CI).<br><br><b>CI:</b> confidence interval; <b>RR:</b> risk ratio; <b>SMD:</b> standardised mean difference</p></td></tr><tr><td align="" colspan="7" rowspan="1"><p><b>GRADE Working Group grades of evidence</b><br><b>High certainty:</b> we are very confident that the true effect lies close to that of the estimate of the effect.<br><b>Moderate certainty:</b> we are moderately confident in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be close to the estimate of the effect, but there is a possibility that it is substantially different.<br><b>Low certainty:</b> our confidence in the effect estimate is limited: the true effect may be substantially different from the estimate of the effect.<br><b>Very low certainty:</b> we have very little confidence in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be substantially different from the estimate of effect.</p></td></tr><tr><td align="" colspan="7" rowspan="1"><p>See interactive version of this table: <a href="https://gdt.gradepro.org/presentations/#/isof/isof_question_revman_web_453424651261012128">https://gdt.gradepro.org/presentations/#/isof/isof_question_revman_web_453424651261012128</a>.</p></td></tr></tbody><tfoot><tr><td colspan="7"><p><sup>a</sup> Downgraded once because concerns about an unclear or high risk of bias in the areas of random sequence generation, allocation concealment, incomplete outcome data and selective reporting.<br><sup>b</sup> Downgraded once due to substantial statistically significant heterogeneity of I² = 61%.<br><sup>c</sup> The lower bound of the 95% confidence interval for the effect estimate reaches the point of clinical significance. Based upon a minimal clinically important difference (MCID) of reduction of 5 points on the Beck Depression Inventory, which is equivalent to a SMD of 0.45 (Button 2015; Hengartner 2022).<br><sup>d</sup> Downgraded twice due to considerable statistically significant heterogeneity of I² = 87%.<br><sup>e</sup> Downgraded once because the lower bound of the 95% confidence interval for the effect estimate does not reach the point of clinical significance. Based upon a MCID of reduction of 5 points on the Beck Depression Inventory which, is equivalent to a SMD of 0.45 (Button 2015; Hengartner 2022).<br><sup>f</sup> Downgraded once due to substantial statistically significant heterogeneity of I² = 74%.<br><sup>g</sup> Downgraded once because the lower bound of the 95% confidence interval for the effect estimate does not reach the point of MCID. Based upon a MCID of reduction of 5 points on the Short‐Form 36 mental component score, which is equivalent to a SMD of 0.25 (Hengartner 2022; Ogura 2020).<br><sup>h</sup> Downgraded once due to moderate non‐statistically significant heterogeneity I² = 44%. One study Brenes 2007 report a non‐statistical significant negative effect.<br><sup>i</sup> Downgraded once because the lower bound of the 95% confidence interval for the effect estimate does not reach the point of MCID. Based upon a MCID of reduction of 5 points on the Short‐Form 36 physical component score, which is equivalent to a SMD of 0.25 (Ogura 2020).<br><sup>j</sup> The study population was limited to community‐dwelling older adults, which may limit the applicability of the findings to other populations.<br><sup>k</sup> Downgraded once because the lower bound of the 95% confidence interval for the effect estimate does not reach the point of MCID. Based upon a MCID of reduction of 5 points on the Short‐Form 36 averaged combined scores, which is equivalent to a SMD of 0.25 (Ogura 2020).</p></td></tr></tfoot></table>

and the <b>relative effect</b> of the intervention (and its 95% CI).<br><br><b>CI:</b> confidence interval; <b>RR:</b> risk ratio; <b>SMD:</b> standardised mean difference</p></td></tr><tr><td align="" colspan="7" rowspan="1"><p><b>GRADE Working Group grades of evidence</b><br><b>High certainty:</b> we are very confident that the true effect lies close to that of the estimate of the effect.<br><b>Moderate certainty:</b> we are moderately confident in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be close to the estimate of the effect, but there is a possibility that it is substantially different.<br><b>Low certainty:</b> our confidence in the effect estimate is limited: the true effect may be substantially different from the estimate of the effect.<br><b>Very low certainty:</b> we have very little confidence in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be substantially different from the estimate of effect.</p></td></tr><tr><td align="" colspan="7" rowspan="1"><p>See interactive version of this table: <a href="https://gdt.gradepro.org/presentations/#/isof/isof_question_revman_web_453430473936187190">https://gdt.gradepro.org/presentations/#/isof/isof_question_revman_web_453430473936187190</a>.</p></td></tr></tbody><tfoot><tr><td colspan="7"><p><sup>a</sup> Downgraded once because concerns about an unclear or high risk of bias in the areas of random sequence generation, allocation concealment, incomplete outcome data, and selective reporting.<br><sup>b</sup> The certainty of the evidence was downgraded due to imprecision, as the confidence interval encompasses both increased and decreased effectiveness, with the possibility of a clinically significant benefit favoring both intervention. Based upon a minimal clinically important difference (MCID) of reduction off 5 points on the Short‐Form 36 mental component score, which is equivalent to a SMD of 0.25 (Hengartner 2022; Ogura 2020).<br><sup>c</sup> The certainty of the evidence was downgraded due to imprecision, as the confidence interval encompasses both increased and decreased effectiveness, with the possibility of a clinically significant benefit favoring psychological therapy. Based upon a MCID of reduction of 5 points on the Short‐Form 36 physical component score, which is equivalent to a SMD of 0.25 (Ogura 2020).<br><sup>d</sup> Downgraded once due to lack of blinding of participants and those delivering the intervention.<br><sup>e</sup> The certainty of evidence was downgraded by two levels for imprecision due to reliance on a single, small RCT with wide confidence intervals that included the possibility of both substantial benefit and minimal or no effect, resulting in considerable uncertainty about the true effect size. Based upon a MCID of reduction of 5 points on the Short‐Form 36 averaged combined scores, which is equivalent to a SMD of 0.25 (Ogura 2020).<br><sup>f</sup> Downgraded by two level for indirectness due to the evidence being derived from a single RCT conducted in Germany with a sample that may not be representative of other contexts. The population was predominantly female (69.2%) with a mean age of 42 years and included both clinical and non‐clinical individuals, limiting applicability to broader or more diverse populations.</p></td></tr></tfoot></table>

musculoskeletal injuries in 3 of 53 participants in the exercise group; five participants from the medication group experienced medication adverse events. Blumenthal (2007) used a 36‐item somatic symptom checklist and found that a few participants reported worsening symptoms. Only one side effect showed a significant group difference (P = 0.03): post‐treatment diarrhoea and loose stools were more common in the sertraline group (31%) than in the home‐based (21%) and supervised exercise (10%) groups. Blumenthal (2012a), using the same checklist, found two significantly different side effects: the sertraline group reported greater post‐treatment fatigue (20%) and sexual problems (26%) compared to the exercise group (2.4% for both). Hidalgo (2021) found adverse events in 8.9% of the exercise group (osteomuscular pain, mild contusions, dizziness) and in 22.5% of the antidepressant group (dizziness 6.5%, epigastric pain 5.2%, drowsiness 3.2%).</p></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p>625<br>(4 RCTs)</p></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p>⊕⊕⊝⊝<br>Low <sup>a,</sup><sup>i</sup></p></td><td align="" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p>Exercise may result in fewer adverse events than pharmacological treatments.</p></td></tr><tr><td align="" colspan="7" rowspan="1"><p>* <b>The risk in the intervention group</b> (and its 95% confidence interval) is based on the assumed risk in the comparison group and the <b>relative effect</b> of the intervention (and its 95% CI).<br><br><b>CI:</b> confidence interval;
